باب القياس
تعريف القياس وحكمه
المذكورة.
تفريعا على ما سيأتي، وهذا هو رأي جمهور الأصوليين اقتداءً بفخر الإسلام له، وقد ابتدع بعض الشارحين فقال : إنه يتضمن ست شروط الأربعة منها هي والاثنان التعدية وكون الحكم الشرعي ثابتا بالنص لا فرعًا لشيء آخر، وهذا وإن كان مما يستقيم لكن ليست له ثمرة صحيحة فلا يستقيم التعليل لإثبات اسم الزنا للواطة؛ لأنه ليس بحكم شرعي، تفريع على أول الشرط، وهو كون الحكم شرعيًا، فإن الشافعي حلبه يقول: الزنا سفح ماءٍ محرم في محل مشتهى محرم، وهذا المعنى موجود في اللواطة، بل هي فوقه في الحرمة والشهوة وتضييع الماء، فيجري عليها اسم الزنا وحكمه وإليه ذهب أبو يوسف ومحمد جمالها، وهذا يسمى قياسًا في اللغة، ولكنه فُرّق بين أن يعطي للواطة اسم الزنا وبين أن يجري عليها حكمه فقط لأجل اشتراك العلة؛ وهذا أي تضمن هذا الشرط أربعة شروط. (القمر) التعدية إلخ المراد بالتعدية أن يثبت حكم الأصل للفرع، وليس المراد به أن ينتقل الحكم من الأصل إلى الفرع فإن الحكم وصف، ونقل الأوصاف محال.(القمر) ومن حكمه الحكم الشرعي: أي الذي في المقيس عليه. (القمر) بالنص : أي الكتاب أو السنة أو الإجماع. (القمر) لا فرعًا إلخ أي لا يكون الحكم الشرعي الذي في المقيس عليه فرعًا لشيء آخر بأن يكون ثابتا لقياس على شيء آخر؛ لأنه لو كان ذلك الحكم الشرعي ثابتا بالقياس فلا بد له من أصل، وهو الشيء الآخر من علته، فيقاس عليه بهذه العلة لا على هذا المقيس عليه الفرع، فإنه تطويل بلا طائل. (القمر) وهذا: أي تضمن هذا الشرط ست شروط. (القمر) لأنه أي لأن إثبات اسم الزنا للواطة. (القمر) بل هي: أي اللواطة فوق أي فوق الزنا في الحرمة، فإن الإيلاج في الدبر لا يحلّ قطعا، بخلاف الإيلاج في القبل فإنه يحل بالنكاح وملك اليمين والشهوة فإن المحل اليابس محل شهوة زائدة. (القمر) فيجري عليها إلخ فيدخل اللائط تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِائَةَ جَلْدَةٍ﴾ (النور: ٢) فيجري عليه حكم الزنا أيضًا، فإن اللواطة حينئذ من أفراد الزنا لغة وقيل إن الشافعي يحلله أيضًا لا يجوز القياس في اللغة، وإنما أوجب الحدّ على اللائط بدلالة النص، لا أنه قياس في اللغة (القمر)
وهذا [أي جريان اسم الزنا على اللواطة أولًا، وجريان حكم الزنا ثانيًا على جريان الاسم يسمى قياسا]. قياسًا في اللغة والقياس في اللغة لا يجوز، وهو عبارة عن أن يوضع لفظ لمسمى مخصوص باعتبار معنى يوجد في غيره فيطلق ذلك اللفظ على ذلك الغير. (القمر)
فإن الأول قياس في اللغة دون الثاني والمجوّزون له هم أكثر أصحاب الشافعي له؛ فإنهم يعطون اسم الخمر لكل ما يُخامر العقل، وقد قال لهم واحد من الحنفية: لِمَ تُسمَّى القارورة قارورةً؟ فقالوا: لأنه يتقرّر فيه الماء، فقال: إن بطنك أيضًا يتقرّر فيه الماء، فينبغي أن يُسمّى قارورةً ثم قال لهم : لمَ يُسمى الجرجير جرجيرًا؟ فقالوا: إنه يتجرجر، أي يتحرك على وجه الأرض، فقال: إن الحيتك أيضاً يتحرّك، فينبغي أن تُسمى جرجيرا، فتحيّر وسكت. ولا لصحة ظهار الذمي تفريع على الشرط الثاني، أي لا يستقيم التعليل لصحة ظهار الذمي كما علّله الشافعي له، فيقول: إنه يصح طلاقه، فيصح ظهاره كالمسلم؛ إذ لم يوجد الشرط الثاني وهو تعدية الحكم بعينه. لكونه أي لكون هذا التعليل تغييرا للحرمة المتناهية بالكفارة في الأصل، وهو المسلم إلى إطلاقها في الفرع عن الغاية؛ لأن ظهار المسلم ينتهي بالكفارة، وظهار الذمي يكون مؤبدا؛ أي إطلاق الحرمة أي الذمي وهي الكفارة. فإن الأول: أي أعطاء اللواطة اسم الزنا. (القمر) دون الثاني: أي إجراء أحكام الزنا على اللواطة (القمر) فإنهم يعطون إلخ: فإن عصير العنب لا يسمى خمراً قبل الشدة، فإذا حصل الشدة يسمى خمرا، فكذا كل ما خامر العقل فهو خمر، فيجرى عليه حكم الخمر قال في "غاية البيان" : يقال خامره، أي خالطه، وقال في "الجمل" في حاشية الجلالين يخامر العقل أي يستره ويغطيه. (القمر) الجرجير إلخ هو ضرب من البقول (السنبلي) على شرط الثاني: أي تعدية حكم الأصل بعينه إلى الفرع.(القمر) كالمسلم أي كظهار المسلم فإن الذمي مكلّف أتى بالقول الزور، ويصح طلاقه فإنه أهل للحرمة، وموجب الظهار ليس إلا الحرمة فيصح ظهاره أيضًا. (القمر)
إذ لم يوجد إلخ دليل لقوله : لا يستقيم إلخ، دليل على استقامة التعليل. (المحشي) تغييرًا إلخ ولك أن تقول: إن مقتضى الظهار الحرمة والكفارة مزيلها والتعليل إنما هو لتعدية الحرمة، فيمكن القول بناءً على أن الكافر مكلف بالأحكام بأن الحرمة تتعدّى إلى الكافر ووجب الكفارة عليه أيضًا، إلا أن أداء الكفارة بسبب كفره لا يمكن فحكم الأصل لم يتغير بل تعدّى بعينه إلى الفرع كذا أفاد بحر العلوم. (القمر) وهو المسلم فإن المسلم من أهل العتاق والإطعام، والصوم. (القمر)
Chapter of Qiyas. Definition of Qiyas and its ruling. As a branch of what will come, and this is the opinion of the majority of usul scholars following Fakhr al-Islam, and some commentators innovated saying: it includes six conditions, four of which are [missing] and the two are transitivity and that the legal ruling is established by text not as a branch of something else. And this, even if it is plausible, has no sound fruit, so the reasoning to establish the name of zina for liwat is not sound because it is not a legal ruling, branching on the first condition, which is that the ruling is legal. For al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) says: Zina is the unlawful emission of water into a desired unlawful place, and this meaning exists in liwat, rather it is above it in prohibition, desire, and waste of water, so the name and ruling of zina applies to it, and this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), and this is called qiyas in language, but it is distinguished between giving the name of zina to liwat and applying its ruling only due to the commonality of the cause. (al-Qamar) 'Transitivity etc.': The meaning of transitivity is that the ruling of the original is established for the branch, not that the ruling transfers from the original to the branch, for the ruling is an attribute, and transferring attributes is impossible. (al-Qamar) 'And from its ruling' the legal ruling: i.e., that which is in the maqis alayh. (al-Qamar) 'By text': i.e., the Book, Sunnah, or Ijma. (al-Qamar) 'Not a branch etc.': i.e., the legal ruling in the maqis alayh should not be a branch of something else, such that it is established by qiyas upon something else; because if that legal ruling is established by qiyas, it must have an original, which is that something else of its cause, then the qiyas is upon that by this cause, not upon this branch maqis alayh, for it is lengthening without benefit. (al-Qamar) 'And this': i.e., this condition includes six conditions. (al-Qamar) 'Because it': i.e., because establishing the name of zina for liwat. (al-Qamar) 'Rather it is': i.e., liwat is above, i.e., above zina in prohibition, for insertion in the anus is absolutely unlawful, unlike insertion in the front which is lawful by marriage and right hand possession, and desire: the dry place is a place of increased desire. (al-Qamar) 'So it applies to it etc.': So the luti enters under His statement: 'The female zani and the male zani, flog each one of them one hundred lashes' (24:2), so the ruling of zina also applies to him, for then liwat is an individual of zina linguistically. And it is said that al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) also holds that qiyas in language is not permissible, and he imposed the hadd on the luti by textual indication, not that it is qiyas in language. (al-Qamar) 'And this [i.e., the application of the name of zina to liwat first, and the application of the ruling of zina second upon the application of the name is called qiyas].' Qiyas in language, and qiyas in language is not permissible, and it is that a word is placed for a specific named thing by virtue of a meaning found in another, so that word is applied to that other. (al-Qamar) 'For the first' is qiyas in language, unlike the second, and those who permit it are most of the companions of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him); for they give the name of khamr to everything that intoxicates the mind. And a Hanafi said to them: Why is the bottle called qarurah? They said: Because water settles in it. He said: Your stomach also has water settling in it, so it should be called qarurah. Then he said to them: Why is jarjir called jarjir? They said: It is jarjar, i.e., it moves on the ground. He said: Your fish also moves, so it should be called jarjir. So they were bewildered and remained silent. And not for the validity of dhihar of the dhimmi, branching on the second condition, i.e., the reasoning for the validity of dhihar of the dhimmi is not sound as al-Shafi'i reasoned, saying: His divorce is valid, so his dhihar is valid like the Muslim, since the second condition, which is the transitivity of the very ruling, is absent. Because this reasoning changes the finite prohibition by expiation in the original, which is the Muslim, to its absolute release in the branch from the goal; because the dhihar of a Muslim ends with expiation, while the dhihar of a dhimmi is perpetual; i.e., absolute prohibition, and the expiation is for the dhimmi? (al-Qamar) 'For the first': i.e., giving the name of zina to liwat. (al-Qamar) 'Unlike the second': i.e., applying the rulings of zina to liwat. (al-Qamar) 'For they give etc.': For grape juice is not called khamr before intoxication, and when intoxication occurs it is called khamr, so likewise everything that intoxicates the mind is khamr, so the ruling of khamr applies to it. In Ghayat al-Bayan it is said: 'Khamarahu' means mixed it. And in al-Jamal in the Hashiya of al-Jalalayn: 'Yukhamiru al-aql' means it covers and conceals it. (al-Qamar) 'Jarjir etc.' is a type of legume. (al-Sanbali) 'On the condition of the second': i.e., transitivity of the ruling of the original itself to the branch. (al-Qamar) 'Like the Muslim': i.e., like the dhihar of a Muslim, for the dhimmi is obligated, uttered false speech, and his divorce is valid, for he is capable of prohibition, and the import of dhihar is only prohibition, so his dhihar is also valid. (al-Qamar) 'Since it is not found etc.': Evidence for his statement 'it is not sound etc.', evidence for the soundness of the reasoning. (al-Muhashi) 'Change etc.': You may say: The requirement of dhihar is prohibition, and expiation removes it, and the reasoning is only for transitivity of prohibition, so one can say based on the fact that the disbeliever is obligated by rulings that the prohibition transfers to the disbeliever and expiation is also obligatory upon him, except that performing expiation due to his disbelief is not possible, so the ruling of the original has not changed but rather transferred itself to the branch. Thus stated Bahr al-Ulum. (al-Qamar) 'And he is the Muslim': for the Muslim is capable of manumission, feeding, and fasting. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar discusses the definition of qiyas (analogy) and its conditions, then delves into a specific dispute about qiyas in language (qiyas fi al-lugha) applied to the case of liwat (sodomy) and zina (adultery), and the case of dhihar (zihar) of a dhimmi. The author first explains that the standard view among Hanafi usul scholars is that qiyas requires certain conditions. Some commentators have stated six conditions, but the author critiques this as having no practical fruit. The main dispute revolves around whether the name and ruling of zina apply to liwat. Imam al-Shafi'i is reported to say that the meaning of zina exists in liwat, so both name and ruling apply. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani agreed with this, but the author distinguishes between applying the name (which is qiyas in language) and applying the ruling. The Hanafi position is that qiyas in language is invalid; one cannot extend a word to a new meaning based on a common attribute. To illustrate, a Hanafi scholar engages in a dialogue with Shafi'is about naming a bottle 'qarurah' because water settles in it, asking why a stomach isn't called a bottle. Similarly, the herb 'jarjir' is named for its motion, so why not call a fish 'jarjir'? The Shafi'is were silenced. The passage then discusses the case of a dhimmi's dhihar. Al-Shafi'i analogized it to the Muslim's dhihar because divorce is valid, so dhihar should be valid. The Hanafi objection is that the ruling of dhihar for a Muslim is finite, ending with expiation, while for a dhimmi it is perpetual because expiation is not valid from him. Thus the analogy fails because the ruling in the branch is not identical to the ruling in the original. This demonstrates the Hanafi condition that the ruling must be transferred 'with its very self' (bi 'aynihi), not changed. The commentary by 'al-Qamar' and 'al-Muhashi' provides further elaboration on these points, affirming the Hanafi rejection of qiyas in language and emphasizing that true analogy requires the ruling to be identical, not modified.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in Islamic law: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a common effective cause (illah).
القياس في اللغة (al-qiyas fi al-lugha): Linguistic analogy: extending a word from its original meaning to a new referent based on a common attribute, without a new linguistic convention.
التعدية (al-ta'diyah): Transitivity: the condition that the ruling of the original case is extended to the branch case without change.
دلالة النص (dalalat al-nass): Textual indication: inferring a ruling from the text's context or underlying rationale, without explicit mention. It is distinct from qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the hadd for liwat (sodomy) not derived from qiyas in language on zina?
A) Because liwat is not a crime in Islam.
B) Because qiyas in language is invalid; the hadd is established by textual indication (dalalat al-nass).
C) Because Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagreed with the hadd for liwat.
D) Because the verse on zina only applies to women.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas in language (extending names based on shared meanings) is invalid in Hanafi usul; the ruling must be transferred identically (ta'diyah bi 'aynihi) for a legal analogy to be sound.

Definition of Qiyas and Hanafi Refutation of Linguistic Analogy
Definition of qiyas and a dispute on linguistic analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between linguistic and technical qiyas, and apply the Hanafi argument to reject linguistic analogy as a source of law.

Core Idea
Linguistic analogy is invalid for legal rulings — only technical qiyas with precise conditions is accepted.

This lesson opens the chapter on qiyas in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It defines qiyas and immediately confronts a critical dispute: can the name and ruling of khamr be extended to all intoxicants via linguistic analogy? The Hanafi school answers with a firm no, using a reductio ad absurdum to expose the weakness of pure linguistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qiyas
The text opens by distinguishing linguistic qiyas (first) from technical qiyas (second). Determine which one is accepted in Hanafi usul.
The Hanafi school accepts only technical qiyas with specific conditions.
2
State the Shafi'i position on linguistic qiyas
The majority of al-Shafi'i's companions accept linguistic analogy. Identify their example: extending the name and ruling of khamr to all intoxicants because the common attribute is 'covering the intellect'.
The Shafi'i position uses the idea that the linguistic meaning includes the 'illa.
3
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
A Hanafi scholar presents two analogies: the bottle (qarura) and the rocket plant (jarjir). He shows that if pure linguistic analogy were valid, absurd consequences would follow.
This is a reductio ad absurdum (takhalluf an al-'illa).
4
Analyze the refutation method
The Hanafi argument demonstrates that linguistic meaning alone is insufficient for legal rulings. The correct method requires a precise 'illa derived from Shar'i evidence.
Technical qiyas is governed by strict conditions (tahrir al-manat, etc.).
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi fiqh, only khamr (from grapes) is najis and prohibited in small amounts; other intoxicants are prohibited but not najis. In Shafi'i fiqh, all intoxicating liquids are khamr and najis.
This difference stems from the acceptance or rejection of linguistic analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس. تعريف القياس وحكمه. فإن الأول قياس في اللغة دون الثاني والمجوّزون له هم أكثر أصحاب الشافعي له؛ فإنهم يعطون اسم الخمر لكل ما يُخامر العقل، وقد قال لهم واحد من الحنفية: لِمَ تُسمَّى القارورة قارورةً؟ فقالوا: لأنه يتقرّر فيه الماء، فقال: إن بطنك أيضًا يتقرّر فيه الماء، فينبغي أن يُسمّى قارورةً ثم قال لهم : لمَ يُسمى الجرجير جرجيرًا؟ فقالوا: إنه يتجرجر، أي يتحرك على وجه الأرض، فقال: إن الحيتك أيضاً يتحرّك، فينبغي أن تُسمى جرجيرا، فتحيّر وسكت.
Chapter of Qiyas. Definition of Qiyas and its ruling. For the first is qiyas in language without the second, and those who permit it are most of the companions of al-Shafi'i; for they give the name of wine to everything that covers the intellect. And a Hanafi said to them: Why is the bottle called qarura? They said: Because water settles in it. He said: Your stomach also water settles in it, so it should be called qarura. Then he said to them: Why is the rocket plant called jarjir? They said: Because it moves (yatajarjar) on the ground. He said: Your fish also moves, so it should be called jarjir. They were confused and fell silent.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on qiyas (analogy) in usul al-fiqh. It first distinguishes between linguistic qiyas (general analogy in language) and technical qiyas (the specific juristic method). The text then presents a debate between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Shafi'i school permits using linguistic analogy to extend the name and ruling of khamr (wine) to any intoxicating substance based on the common attribute of covering the intellect. A Hanafi refutes this by showing that such reasoning leads to absurd results if applied consistently, like calling a bottle 'qarura' because water settles in it (since the stomach also settles water), or calling a fish 'jarjir' because it moves on the ground. This demonstrates that linguistic analogy is not a valid source of law in the Hanafi school; only technical qiyas with precise conditions is accepted.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Analogy in usul al-fiqh; here distinguished between linguistic and technical qiyas.
القياس في اللغة (al-qiyas fi al-lugha): Analogy based on linguistic meanings, e.g., extending a name based on a common attribute.
خمر (khamr): Wine from grapes; in the text, the point of dispute is whether the name extends to all intoxicants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the 'illa of covering the intellect is present.
B) No, because linguistic analogy is not a valid source of law in the Hanafi school.
C) Yes, because the Shafi'i school permits it.
D) No, because intoxicants are not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects linguistic analogy as a source of law; only technical qiyas with a precise 'illa derived from Shar'i evidence is valid.

The Second Condition of Qiyas: Zihar of a Dhimmi
Refuting the Shafi'i analogy for zihar of a dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the second condition of qiyas (identical transmission of ruling) and apply it to evaluate the validity of the analogy between a dhimmi's zihar and his divorce.

Core Idea
For qiyas to be valid, the ruling must be transmitted identically; any change invalidates the analogy.

This passage examines a specific usul dispute about the validity of zihar for a dhimmi. The Shafi'i school argues that since a dhimmi's divorce is valid, his zihar should also be valid by analogy with a Muslim. The Hanafi response reveals a critical flaw: the ruling changes from finite (ended by kaffarah) to permanent, violating the second condition of qiyas. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed analogy and its proponents
The Shafi'i school argues that just as a dhimmi's divorce is valid, his zihar should also be valid, by analogy with a Muslim. Recognize that analogy is a form of qiyas, requiring specific conditions.
2
Recall the second condition of qiyas
The second condition states that the ruling (hukm) must be transmitted identically from the original case (asl) to the branch case (far'). Any change in the ruling—whether in duration, obligation, or consequence—breaks the analogy.
This condition is accepted by both Hanafi and Shafi'i schools, but its application is disputed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection
In the original case (Muslim's zihar), the ruling is that the prohibition is finite and ends upon performing kaffarah (expiation). In the branch case (dhimmi's zihar), the dhimmi cannot perform kaffarah because it combines worship and punishment, so the prohibition becomes permanent. Thus the ruling changes, violating the second condition.
The change from finite to permanent is a qualitative change in the ruling itself.
4
Understand why a dhimmi cannot perform kaffarah
Kaffarah (emancipating a slave or fasting) involves the intention of worship (niyyah), which a dhimmi is not obligated to perform. Even if he could free a slave, the alternative of fasting is an act of worship incompatible with his status.
Some opinions hold that a dhimmi is eligible for emancipation but not for the substitution by fasting.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The Hanafi position: a dhimmi's zihar is invalid—it does not occur. The Shafi'i position: a dhimmi's zihar is valid, but the required kaffarah is not accepted from him, leaving him in a perpetual state of sin.
This illustrates how a difference in usul leads to divergent fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لصحة ظهار الذمي تفريع على الشرط الثاني، أي لا يستقيم التعليل لصحة ظهار الذمي كما علّله الشافعي له، فيقول: إنه يصح طلاقه، فيصح ظهاره كالمسلم؛ إذ لم يوجد الشرط الثاني وهو تعدية الحكم بعينه. لكونه أي لكون هذا التعليل تغييرا للحرمة المتناهية بالكفارة في الأصل، وهو المسلم إلى إطلاقها في الفرع عن الغاية؛ لأن ظهار المسلم ينتهي بالكفارة، وظهار الذمي يكون مؤبدا؛ أي إطلاق الحرمة أي الذمي وهي الكفارة. إذ ليس هو أهلًا للكفارة التي هي دائرة بين العبادة والعقوبة، وقيل: هو أهل للتحرير ولكن ليس أهلا للتحرير الذي يخلفه الصوم.
And not for the validity of zihar of the dhimmi, a branch on the second condition, meaning the reasoning for the validity of zihar of the dhimmi is not sound as al-Shafi'i reasoned, for he says: His divorce is valid, so his zihar is valid like the Muslim; because the second condition is not found, which is transmitting the ruling identically. Because this reasoning is a change of the finite prohibition by expiation in the original (the Muslim) to absolute prohibition in the branch without termination; because the zihar of the Muslim ends with expiation, and the zihar of the dhimmi becomes permanent; meaning absolute prohibition for the dhimmi, and expiation. Because he is not fit for expiation which is between worship and punishment; and it is said: he is fit for emancipation but not fit for the emancipation that is substituted by fasting.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage discusses the second condition of valid qiyas: the ruling must be transmitted identically from the original to the branch. The Shafi'i school argues that since a dhimmi's divorce is valid, his zihar should also be valid by analogy (qiyas) with a Muslim. The Hanafi response is that the analogy fails because the ruling in the original (Muslim's zihar) is finite—it ends with expiation (kaffarah). In the branch (dhimmi's zihar), the ruling would be permanent since the dhimmi cannot perform expiation (as he is not capable of the worship aspect). This changes the ruling, violating the condition of identical transmission. The dhimmi is not qualified for expiation because it combines worship and punishment; he cannot perform worship with intent. Thus, the analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a man declares his wife like his mother's back; it is a sin requiring expiation (kaffarah).
الشرط الثاني (al-shart al-thani): The second condition of qiyas: the ruling must be transmitted identically without change from the original to the branch.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; in the context of zihar, it involves emancipating a slave or fasting two consecutive months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the analogy establishing the validity of zihar for a dhimmi?
A) Because a dhimmi cannot pronounce divorce.
B) Because the ruling for a Muslim's zihar ends with expiation, but a dhimmi's zihar would be permanent, changing the ruling.
C) Because a dhimmi is not a legally competent person.
D) Because zihar is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The second condition of qiyas requires the ruling to be transmitted identically; any change in the nature of the ruling invalidates the analogy.

Refuting the Shafi'i Analogy: Third Condition of Qiyas
Refuting the Shafi'i analogy that coerced and mistaken persons are more deserving of excuse than the forgetful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Shafi'i analogy extending the ruling of the forgetful to the mistaken and coerced, and apply the third condition of qiyas to determine when a branch is not a proper parallel.

Core Idea
The forgetful person's excuse is stronger because his action is attributed to God, not to his own choice or negligence.

In this passage, the Hanafi school defends a critical condition of qiyas: the branch must be truly similar to the original. The Shafi'i argument that the mistaken and coerced are more excused than the forgetful seems plausible at first, but a deeper analysis of intention and attribution reveals a fundamental distinction. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly applying qiyas in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original and Branch
First, identify the original case (the forgetful person who eats while fasting, whose fast is not broken) and the branch cases (the mistaken person who swallows water accidentally and the coerced person who eats under duress). Note the ruling in the original: the fast remains valid.
The original's ruling is established by a specific proof (hadith).
2
State the Shafi'i Analogy
The Shafi'i school uses an argument a fortiori: if the forgetful, who intentionally eats, is excused, then those who lack intention (mistaken and coerced) deserve excuse more. Understand this reasoning.
This is an analogy based on the attribute of intention.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafis reject the analogy by showing that the branch is not similar. The forgetful's action is attributed to God (hadith: 'it is God who fed him'), while the mistaken and coerced act from their own choices (negligence or response to coercion). Their excuses are weaker.
Attribution to the Lawgiver is a unique quality of forgetfulness.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, the mistake or coerced person's fast is broken and must be made up; in the Shafi'i school, it remains valid. Recognize how this difference arises from the usul principle.
The dispute hinges on the third condition of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لتعدية الحكم من الناسي في الفطر إلى المكره والخاطئ؛ لأن عذرهما دون عذره، تفريع على الشرط الثالث، وهو كون الفرع نظيرًا للأصل؛ فإن الشافعي محله يقول: لما عُذر الناسي أي ناسي الصوم مع كونه عامدًا في نفس الفعل فَلَأن يُعدّر الخاطئ والمكره وهما ليسا بعامدين في نفس الفعل أولى، ونحن نقول: إن عذرهما دون عذره؛ فإن النسيان يقع بلا اختيار، وهو منسوب إلى صاحب الحق، وفعل الخاطئ والمكره من غير صاحب الحق، فإن الخاطئ يذكر الصوم ولكنه يقصر في الاحتياط في المضمضة حتى دخل الماء في حلقه، والمكره أكرهه الإنسان، وألجأه إليه، فلم يكن عذرهما كعذر الناسي، فيفسد صومهما، وقد فرعناهما فيما سبق على كون الأصل مخالفا للقياس، ولا ضير فيه؛ فإن أكثر المسائل يتفرع على أصول مختلفة.
And not for transmitting the ruling from the forgetful regarding breaking the fast to the coerced and the mistaken; because their excuse is less than his excuse, a branch on the third condition, which is that the branch be similar to the original. For al-Shafi'i says: Since the forgetful person (i.e., one who forgets fasting while being intentional in the act itself) is excused, then it is more appropriate that the mistaken and the coerced, who are not intentional in the act itself, be excused. And we say: Their excuse is less than his excuse; for forgetfulness occurs without choice, and it is attributed to the owner of the right (the Lawgiver), while the act of the mistaken and the coerced is from other than the owner of the right. For the mistaken person remembers the fast but is careless in precaution in rinsing until water entered his throat, and the coerced person was coerced by a human and compelled to it. So their excuse is not like the excuse of the forgetful, so their fast is invalidated. And we have branched them previously on the basis that the original is contrary to qiyas, and there is no harm in that; for most issues branch on different principles.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage addresses the third condition of valid qiyas: that the branch must be a proper parallel (nazir) to the original. The Shafi'i school argues by analogy of the greater: if the forgetful person (who intentionally performs the act of eating but forgets he is fasting) is excused and his fast is not broken, then the mistaken (khati') and coerced (mukrah) persons, who are not intentional in the act, are more deserving of excuse. The Hanafi response distinguishes their excuses: forgetfulness occurs without any choice and is attributed to the Lawgiver (God), as the Prophet ﷺ said about the forgetful person. The mistaken person, however, remembers his fast but fails to take precaution (e.g., in rinsing), so his action stems from his own negligence. The coerced person chooses to break the fast to avoid harm from a human coercer, so his action is also from himself. Thus, their excuses are weaker, and their fast is invalidated. The passage also notes that these rulings were previously derived from different origins (usul), which is acceptable.

Key Terms
الشرط الثالث (al-shart al-thalith): The third condition of qiyas: the branch must be similar (nazir) to the original in the relevant aspect.
ناسي (nasi): One who forgets; specifically here, one who eats while forgetting he is fasting.
خاطئ (khati'): One who makes a mistake; here, one who accidentally breaks the fast due to lack of precaution.
مكره (mukrah): One who is coerced; here, one who eats under duress from another person.

The third condition of qiyas—requiring the branch to be a true parallel to the original—prevents extending the excuse of the forgetful to the mistaken or coerced, because their actions are qualitatively different in attribution and responsibility.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Third Condition of Qiyas: When Is the Branch Not Analogous? (Nasi vs Mukrah/Khata')
Usul dispute on the third condition of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments regarding the extension of the forgetful faster's ruling to the coerced and mistaken, and apply the third condition of qiyas (verification of branch similarity) to determine the validity of analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
The nasi's excuse is uniquely attributed to Allah; thus the branch (mukrah/khata') is not analogous, so qiyas fails.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute on the third condition of qiyas: the branch (far') must be analogous (nazir) to the root (asl). The Shafi'i school argues that if the forgetful faster (nasi) is excused, then the coerced (mukrah) and mistaken (khata') should be even more excused. The Hanafi response refutes this by showing that the nasi's excuse is fundamentally different because his act is attributed directly to Allah. This lesson guides you through the reasoning, the identification of the condition's application, and the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root and its ruling
The root (asl) is the case of the forgetful faster (nasi) who eats or drinks forgetfully. The ruling is that his fast remains valid and he need not make it up (according to the hadith 'Allah fed you and gave you drink'). This is the established ruling from the Shari'ah.
2
Identify the branch and the proposed extension
The branch cases are the coerced (mukrah) and the mistaken (khata') faster who break the fast. The Shafi'i argues that since the nasi is excused despite being intentional in the act of eating/drinking, then the mukrah and khata', who have less or no intention, should be excused a fortiori (awlā). Thus the ruling of validity should be extended from the nasi to them via qiyas.
The Shafi'i uses the principle that the more deserving case should receive the same ruling.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: unique nature of forgetfulness
The Hanafis reject the analogy by emphasizing that the nasi's excuse is unique: forgetfulness occurs without choice and the act is attributed to the Owner of the right (Allah) based on the hadith. In contrast, the mistaken and coerced act from their own shortcoming or external compulsion; their acts are not attributed to Allah. Thus the branch is not analogous to the root; the third condition of qiyas (the branch must be similar) is not met.
The Hanafi response focuses on the cause ('illa) being specific to the nasi.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute has a direct fiqhi outcome: in the Hanafi school, the fast of the coerced and mistaken is invalidated and requires qada (make-up). In the Shafi'i school, they are excused like the forgetful, so their fast remains valid.
This is the applied difference between the two schools.
5
Evaluate the epistemological weight
Note that the Hanafi position relies on a precise understanding of qiyas conditions and the nature of divine attribution. The Shafi'i argument uses a general principle of a fortiori reasoning. The disagreement is methodological, not just textual.
This shows how usul disputes can generate different fiqh rulings.

Commentary
This is a usul dispute regarding the third condition of qiyas: the branch must be analogous to the root. The Shafi'i argues that since the forgetful person (nasi) in fasting is excused despite being intentional in the act of eating/drinking, then a fortiori the mistaken (khata') and coerced (mukrah) should be excused. The Hanafi response distinguishes that forgetfulness is involuntary and attributed directly to Allah, while the mistaken and coerced act from their own shortcoming or external coercion but not from a divine cause. Thus, the branch is not analogous to the root, so qiyas cannot extend the ruling. The text also notes that the asl (the ruling on the forgetful person) itself is contrary to qiyas (an exception), but this does not prevent branching from it.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh: extending a ruling from an asl to a far' based on shared illa.
الفرع (al-far'): The branch case to which the ruling is extended via qiyas.
الأصل (al-asl): The root case that has a known ruling from nass.
الناسي (al-nasi): One who forgets; specifically the fasting person who eats/drinks forgetfully.
المكره (al-mukrah): One who is coerced into an action.
الخاطئ (al-khata'): One who errs unintentionally.

Key Takeaway
For qiyas to be valid, the branch must be analogous to the root in the relevant cause; the forgetful faster's unique excuse (divine attribution) cannot be extended to the coerced or mistaken.

The Condition of Continuity in Qiyas and the Food Hadith Dispute
Defining qiyas and defending the Hanafi reading of the food hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition that qiyas does not change the original ruling's meaning, and apply the Hanafi and Shafi'i interpretations of the food hadith to determine the permissibility of small-quantity food sales.

Core Idea
Qiyas extends a ruling without altering its essence; the food hadith's exception is on the condition, not the subject.

This passage clarifies a key condition of qiyas: the original ruling's meaning must remain unchanged, only its scope widens. It then defends the Hanafi view that a handful of food can be sold for a handful because the hadith's exception applies to the condition of equality, not to the food itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of qiyas being discussed
The author states that the fourth condition (continuity of the original ruling) means the ruling does not change in its essential content, only that it extends to cover the branch case.
This distinguishes Hanafi qiyas from a mere analogy that might alter the ruling.
2
Understand the hypothetical objection
An objector claims that by using qiyas to limit the riba prohibition to large quantities, the Hanafis have changed the ruling of the hadith, which seems to forbid all unequal food sales.
The objection challenges the very condition of continuity.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using the exception
The Hanafis argue that the exception 'except equal for equal' indicates that the prohibition only applies where equality is relevant—i.e., large quantities. Small quantities are outside the scope of the hadith.
The exception reveals the generality of the first part is restricted to cases where equality can be actualized.
4
Compare Hanafi and Shafi'i interpretations
Imam al-Shafi'i interprets the exception as applying to the food itself: only equal food is exempt. Hanafis interpret it as applying to the condition: only the state of equality is exempted, leaving small quantities unmentioned.
Both require ta'wil; Hanafis prefer theirs because it aligns with default permissibility.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
The difference is whether selling a handful of wheat for two handfuls is permissible (Hanafi) or prohibited (Shafi'i). This illustrates how a subtle usul analysis yields concrete fiqh differences.
The author sides with the Hanafi view, concluding 'so it remains on the original ruling of permissibility.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس
تعريف القياس وحكمه
فأطلق الرابع تنبيها على أنه شرط واحد ومعنى بقاء حكم النص أن لا يتغير عما كان الثالث مع ما تضمنه عليه سوى أنه تعدى إلى الفرع فعمّ.
وإنما خصصنا القليل من قوله علي: "لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء"،* جواب سؤال مقدر ، وهو أنكم قلتم أن لا يتغيّر حكم الأصل بعد التعليل، وفي قوله عليه: "لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام" لما علّلتم حرمة الربا بالقدر والجنس، وعدّيتم إلى غير الطعام، فقد خصصتم القليل من النص الدال على حرمة الربا في القليل والكثير ، وأقصرتم حرمة الربا على الكثير فقط؟ فأجاب بأنا إنما خصصنا القليل من هذا النص؛ لأن استثناء حالة التساوي دل على عموم صدره في الأحوال، ولن يثبت ذلك إلا في الكثير ، يعني إن المساواة مصدر، أن المساواة وهو المراد بقوله: سواء بسواء. وقد وقع مستثنى من الطعام في الظاهر، ولا يصلح أن يكون مستثنى منه في الحقيقة، فلابد من تأويل في أحدهما ؛ فالشافعي لله يأوّل في المستثنى ويقول: معناه لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا طعامًا مساويًا بطعام مساو، فالطعام المساوي بالمساوي صار حلالا، وما سواه كله يبقى حرامًا، فبيع الحفنة بالحفنة وكذا بالحفنتين داخل تحت الحرمة، وهي الأصل في أي الحرمة الأشياء عنده. ونحن نؤوّل في المستثنى منه، ونقدّر :هكذا لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام في حال من الأحوال إلا في حال المساواة والأحوال ثلاثة وهي المساواة، والمفاضلة، والمجازفة، وكلها أحوال الكثير ، فتحلّ منه المساواة، وتحرم المفاضلة والمجازفة، والقليل غير متعرّض به أصلا، لا في المستثنى ولا في المستثنى منه؛ فبقي على الأصل الذي هو الإباحة، فيجوز الحفنة بالحفنة وكذا بالحفنتين لا يقال : إن القلة أيضًا ،حال فتبقى في المستثنى منه،
Chapter of Qiyas. Definition of Qiyas and its ruling. He (the author) mentioned the fourth [condition] as a reminder that it is one condition. The meaning of the continuity of the ruling of the text is that it does not change from what it was in the third [condition] along with what it entailed, except that it extends to the branch and thus becomes general. And we only particularized the small [quantity] from his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal' in response to a hypothetical question, which is: You said that the ruling of the original does not change after the cause [is established], but in his statement: 'Do not sell food for food' when you cause the prohibition of riba to be by measure and type, and you extend it to other than food, you have particularized the small [quantity] from the text indicating the prohibition of riba in both small and large [quantities], and you restricted the prohibition of riba to only large [quantities]? So he answered: We only particularized the small [quantity] from this text because the exception of the condition of equality indicates the generality of its beginning in all conditions, and that is only established in large [quantities], meaning that equality is a source, equality is what is meant by his statement: 'equal for equal'. And it [equality] appears as an exception from food, but in reality it is not suitable to be an exception from it, so it is necessary to interpret one of the two [terms]. Al-Shafi'i interprets in the excepted [term] and says: Its meaning is 'Do not sell food for food except equal food for equal food.' So equal food for equal becomes permissible, and everything else remains prohibited. Thus selling a handful for a handful and similarly two handfuls falls under prohibition, and prohibition is the original ruling for things according to him. And we interpret in the excepted-from [term] and we estimate: 'Do not sell food for food in any condition except in the condition of equality.' And the conditions are three: equality, superiority, and uncertainty [in measure]. All of these are conditions of large quantities. So equality from it is permissible, and superiority and uncertainty are prohibited. The small quantity is not addressed at all, neither in the exception nor in the excepted-from; thus it remains on the original [ruling] which is permissibility. So selling a handful for a handful and similarly two handfuls is permissible. It cannot be said: 'Smallness is also a condition, so it remains in the excepted-from.'
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul. The author first clarifies that the condition of 'continuity of the text's ruling' does not mean the ruling itself changes, but rather that the ruling extends from the original case to a branch case, thereby becoming more general. He then addresses a specific objection: if the hadith forbids selling food for food except in equal measure, how can Hanafis use qiyas to restrict the prohibition to large quantities while allowing small quantities? The objection is that qiyas would change the apparent general ruling. The Hanafi response is that the exception of 'equal for equal' proves that the generality of the first part applies only to cases where equality can be actualized, which is only in large quantities measured by customary standards. The heart of the passage is the classic Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over interpreting the exception in the hadith. Imam al-Shafi'i interprets the exception as pertaining to the subject (food), so that only exactly equal food items are exempt, leaving all other food sales, including small quantities, prohibited. The Hanafis, however, interpret the exception as pertaining to the condition (the state of equality), so that equality is one of three possible states (equality, excess, uncertainty), all of which relate to large quantities. Small quantities, being outside the scope of measurement, are not addressed by the hadith and remain permissible by default. This demonstrates how Hanafi usul uses textual analysis (takhsis, ta'wil) and the principle that the default ruling for transactions is permissibility.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in jurisprudence: extending a ruling from an original case to a branch case due to a common effective cause (illah).
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Particularization: restricting the generality of a text to a subset of its referents.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception: a construction that excludes something from the generality of a statement.
الحال (al-hal): Condition or circumstance: the state in which an action takes place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith 'لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء', what is the ruling on selling a handful of wheat for two handfuls of wheat?
A) Prohibited because it involves riba (excess).
B) Permissible because small quantities are not covered by the hadith.
C) Permissible only if equal in measure.
D) Prohibited because both handfuls are food of the same type.

Key Takeaway
The condition of continuity in qiyas ensures the original ruling's essence remains; the Hanafi interpretation of the food hadith applies this condition, permitting small-quantity sales.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
Defending the Hanafi Ruling on Zakat Value: Qiyas on the Shoulders of Nass
Responding to an objection that qiyas annuls the text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between change by nass and change by qiyas in the zakat value case, and identify the role of the lam of consequence.

Core Idea
The text itself permits substitution; qiyas only accompanies, never overrides.

The permissibility of giving the value of the sheep rather than the sheep itself in Zakat has been contested. This passage meets the objection head-on, clarifying that the Hanafi position does not replace the text with qiyas but rather draws on the broader textual indications about divine provision and the nature of Zakat as a right of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Recognize that the opponent claims allowing value (qima) instead of the specified sheep annuls the explicit text (nass) because the text commands 'one sheep'.
The opponent sees the change as qiyas overriding nass.
2
Identify the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi refutes that the change is by the text itself (dalalat al-nusus) concerning Allah's guarantee of provision for all creatures, and that qiyas (the cause) only accompanies the text, not drives the change.
The key phrase: 'فصار التغيير بالنص أي بدلالة النص حال كونه مصاحبًا للتعليل'.
3
Analyze the Role of Exception (Istithna)
The exception of 'a case of equality' in the hadith shows that the text's generality was never absolute; it only covers majority situations (ahwal al-kathir), not the rare case of equality.
استثناء حالة المساواة يدل على أن الصدر عام في الأحوال المجانسة المناسبة
4
Apply the Lam Distinction
The lam in 'lil-fuqara' is a lam of consequence (lam al-‘aqiba), not possession (tamlik). Zakat is a right of Allah, so the poor do not own it initially; the change in value does not affect their right because the obligation is to fulfill Allah's promise of provision.
The Hanafi view contrasts with the Shafi'i view that the poor own the Zakat directly.
5
Summarize the Thamarah
The practical difference: Hanafi permits giving value; Shafi'i requires the specific item. The Hanafi ruling is based on the textual indication of divine provision, not mere analogy.
This clarifies that the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the text's scope.

فتكون حرامًا؛ لأنا نقول: إنها حال بعيد غير متداول في العرف، والأقرب بالمساواة هو الحال التي للكثير، فلا يُراد بالمستثنى منه إلا أحوال الكثير لا القليل، فصار التغيير بالنص أي بدلالة النص حال كونه مصاحبًا للتعليل، لا به، أي بالتعليل كما ظننتم. وإنما سقط حق الفقير في الصورة، جواب سؤال آخر تقريره: أن الشرع أوجب الشاة في زكاة السوائم حيث قال عليه : " في خمس من الإبل شاة"، وأنتم علّلتم صلاحيتها للفقير بأنها مال صالح للحوائج وكل ما كان كذلك يجوز أداؤه، فيجوز أداء القيمة أيضًا إليه، فأبطلتم قيد الشاة المفهومة من النص صريحًا؟ فأجاب بأنه إنما سقط حق الفقير في صورة الشاة، وتعدّى إلى القيمة بالنص لا بالتعليل؛ لأن الله تعالى وعد أرزاق الفقراء، إنها أي القلة حال بعيد إلخ لأن استثناء حالة المساواة يدل على أن الصدر عام في الأحوال المجانسة المناسبة لهذه الحالة مجانسة قريبة بأن يكون تلك الأحوال مبنية على المعيار الشرعي، فلا يكون تلك الأحوال إلا أحوال الكثير بخلاف القلة، فإنها لا تجانس حالة المساواة مجانسة قريبة فلا تدخل في عموم الأحوال. (السنبلي) عللتم صلاحيتها إلخ أي بينتم علة كون الشاة صالحة للفقير أنها مال صالح للحوائج المختلفة بأن يبيعها الفقير ونفق ثمنها في حاجة أي حاجة كانت وقيمتها أيضًا كذلك، أي صالحة لرفع الحاجة، فحكمها ينبغي أيضا أن يكون كذلك. (القمر) بالنص : أي بدلالة النصوص الواردة في كفالة رزق العباد وإيجاب الزكاة في أموال الأغنياء وصرفها إلى الفقراء. (القمر) تخريجه .

(هود: ٦) بل أرزاق تمام العالم في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا مِنْ دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللَّهِ رِزْقُهَا﴾، وقسّم لكل واحد منهم طرق المعاش، فأعطى الأغنياء من الزراعة والتجارة والكسب. ثم أوجب مالاً مسمّى على الأغنياء لنفسه، وهو الشاة التي يأخذ الله تعالى أوّلاً في يده كما قيل: الصدقة تقع في كفّ الرحمن قبل أن تقع في كف الفقير ثم أمر الأغنياء بإنجاز المواعيد أي إيفاء المواعيد من ذلك المسمّى الذي أخذه بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ وَالْمَسَاكِينَ﴾ الآية، وبقوله عليه: "خُذها أحد أن الله لم يرزق الفقراء، ولم يُوفِ بعهده في حقهم، بل رزقهم الأغنياء، ولهذا قيل: إن اللام في قوله: لِلْفُقَرَاءِ لام العاقبة لا لام التمليك؛ لأن الله تعالى هو يملكها، من أي الله في يده (التوبة: ٦٠) ،أغنيائهم ، ورُدّها إلى فقرائهم، وإنما فعل كذلك لئلا يتوهم (التوبة: ٦٠) وما من دابة : أي ما يدبّ على الأرض. (القمر) ثم أوجب أي بالنصوص الموجبة للزكاة.(القمر) لنفسه أي حقا لنفسه، ولا حق للفقير في الزكاة أصلاً، ألا ترى أنه لو كان للفقير حق في الزكاة لما حلّ وطء الجارية المشتراة للتجارة بعد الحول قبل أداء الزكاة كالجارية المشتراة للتجارة بعد الحول قبل أداء الزكاة كالجارية المشتركة. (القمر) الصدقة تقع كما قال تعالى: ﴿هُوَ يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ وَيَأْخُذُ الصَّدَقَاتِ﴾ (التوبة: ١٠٤) (المحشي) ثم أمر إلخ أي أمر الله تعالى الأغنياء بصرف الحق الذي له تعالى عليهم إلى الفقراء حتى ينجز مواعيد الله تعالى التي في أرزاق الفقراء من ذلك المسمى الذي أخذه الله تعالى ولا يذهب عليك أن وعد أرزاق الفقراء ثابت على الله، وإيجاب المال المسمى على الأغنياء، فأداؤه باختيارهم، فلو عصت الأغنياء و لم يؤدّوا الواجب يبقى الفقراء بلا رزق، وهذا باطل، فكيف يتحقق إنجاز وعده تعالى بهذا المال المسمى الواجب بل إنجاز وعده تعالى إنما هو بإلقاء طريق طلب المعاش في قلوب الفقراء، وإلقاءه إعطاء قدر من المال تطوعًا أو فرضًا في قلوب الأغنياء. (القمر) المواعيد إلخ: لكن الوعد لمن لا يريد موته من الجوع، فلا يرد موت بعض الناس جوعًا على ذلك، والله أعلم. (السنبلي) ولهذا: أي لأن الزكاة حق الله تعالى كالصلاة، وليس حقا للفقير . (القمر) لام العاقبة يعني أنه صار الواجب الذي هو حق الله تعالى خالصًا بعاقبة الفقراء، وإن لم يكن للفقراء فيه حق ابتداءً. (القمر) لا لام التمليك كما قال الشافعي ليه من أن اللام موضوعة للتمليك فيدل قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ (التوبة: ٦٠) الآية على استحقاق هذه الأصناف بالشركة.(القمر)
قد سبق في حديث معاذ الله أنه قال عليه حين بعثه إلى اليمن: فإن هم أطاعوا لذلك فأعلمهم أن الله قد افترض عليهم صدقة تؤخذ من أغنيائهم وتُردّ على فقرائهم الحديث متفق عليه عن ابن عباس ها. [إشراق الأبصار: ٣٠]
Chapter of Qiyas. Definition of Qiyas and its ruling. So it would be forbidden; because we say: it is a distant situation not common in custom, and the nearest by equality is the situation that is for the majority, so from the excepted term only the majority situations are intended, not the few. So the change is by the text, i.e., by the indication of the text while being accompanied by the cause, not by the cause as you thought. And the right of the poor person is only dropped in the form, in answer to another question whose formulation: that the law obligated the sheep in the zakat of grazing animals where he (peace be upon him) said: 'in five camels, one sheep', and you reasoned its suitability for the poor by it being wealth suitable for needs, and everything like that can be given, so giving its value to him is also permissible, so you have annulled the restriction of the sheep understood from the text explicitly? So he answered that the right of the poor person is only dropped in the form of the sheep, and it transfers to the value by the text, not by the cause; because Allah promised the provisions of the poor. That is, the fewness is a distant situation etc., because the exception of the case of equality indicates that the first part is general in situations that are homogeneous and appropriate to this situation with close homogeneity, such that those situations are built on the legal standard, so those situations are only the majority situations as opposed to the few, for they do not have close homogeneity with the case of equality and thus do not enter into the generality of situations. (al-Qamar) 'So the change...' etc.: this is an explanation of the source of the questioner's mistake, meaning that the change, i.e., the change of the opening statement from absolute generality to the generality of many situations, occurred by the text not by the cause, except that the cause accompanies it and is associated with it, so the association makes the objector think that the change is by the cause, so he proceeded to object. The aspect of association is that the exception indicates the non-intention of the few, and the causation by measure and type also indicates that it is not a locus for riba, so they agree. (al-Qamar) 'So the change...' etc. Summary of the answer: specification did not occur here; rather, the generality of the text was only in the cases of measure, and the cause has no entry in them, so understand this summary from al-Tanwir. (al-Sunbuli) 'You reasoned its suitability...' etc., meaning you explained the cause of the sheep being suitable for the poor: that it is wealth suitable for various needs such that the poor person sells it and spends its price on any need, and its value is also like that, i.e., suitable for fulfilling the need, so its ruling should also be like that. (al-Sunbuli) 'So giving its value to him is also permissible' i.e., to the poor person even if the poor person is not satisfied. (al-Qamar) 'So you have annulled...' etc., and this is an annulment of the text's ruling. (al-Qamar) 'So he answered...' etc., and it is possible to answer that the permissibility of spending the value of the named property in zakat is also established in the law, so we have not annulled the restriction of the sheep; rather, the Lawgiver permitted us to do so, as is said. (al-Qamar) 'By the text': i.e., by the indication of the texts dealing with guaranteeing the provisions of servants and obligating zakat on the wealth of the rich and spending it on the poor. (al-Qamar) Its derivation. (Hud: 6) Rather, the provisions of the entire world are in His statement: 'And there is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision', and He divided for each of them the means of livelihood, so He gave the rich from agriculture, trade, and earnings. Then He obligated a named property upon the rich for Himself, which is the sheep that Allah first takes in His hand as it is said: charity falls into the hand of the Merciful before it falls into the hand of the poor. Then He commanded the rich to fulfill the promises, i.e., to fulfill the promises from that named property which He took, by His statement: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor and the needy' (al-Tawba: 60), and by his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Take it, that Allah did not provide for the poor and did not fulfill His covenant regarding them, but the rich provided for them, and for this it is said: the lam in 'for the poor' is the lam of consequence, not the lam of possession; because Allah owns it, from Allah in His hand (al-Tawba: 60), their rich, and return it to their poor. And He only did this lest it be thought (al-Tawba: 60). 'And what creature' means what crawls on earth. (al-Qamar) 'Then He obligated' i.e., by the texts that obligate zakat. (al-Qamar) 'For Himself' i.e., a right for Himself, and the poor have no right in zakat at all. Do you not see that if the poor had a right in zakat, intercourse with a slave girl purchased for trade after a year before paying zakat would not be permitted, like a jointly owned slave girl. (al-Qamar) 'Charity falls' as He said: 'He accepts repentance from His servants and takes charities' (al-Tawba: 104) (al-Muhashi). 'Then He commanded' etc., meaning Allah commanded the rich to spend the right that He has over them to the poor so that they fulfill Allah's promises regarding the provisions of the poor from that named property which Allah took. And it should not escape you that the promise of the poor's provisions is established upon Allah, and the obligation of the named property upon the rich, so its fulfillment is by their choice. If the rich disobey and do not pay the obligatory, the poor would remain without provision, and this is false. So how is the fulfillment of His promise realized by this obligated named property? Rather, the fulfillment of His promise is only by instilling the way of seeking livelihood in the hearts of the poor, and instilling giving a certain amount of wealth voluntarily or as an obligation in the hearts of the rich. (al-Qamar) 'The promises' etc.: but the promise is to those who do not intend to die of hunger, so the death of some people from hunger does not contradict that, and Allah knows best. (al-Sunbuli) 'And for this' i.e., because zakat is the right of Allah like prayer, and is not the right of the poor. (al-Qamar) 'Lam of consequence' means that the obligatory which is the right of Allah becomes purely for the benefit of the poor as a consequence, even though the poor have no right to it initially. (al-Qamar) 'Not the lam of possession' as al-Shafi'i said, because the lam is placed for possession, so His statement: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor' (al-Tawba: 60) indicates the entitlement of these categories by sharing. (al-Qamar) It has already preceded in the hadith of Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him) that he (peace be upon him) said when he sent him to Yemen: 'If they obey you in that, then inform them that Allah has prescribed upon them a charity taken from their rich and returned to their poor.' The hadith is agreed upon from Ibn Abbas. [Ishraq al-Absar: 30]
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of an objection raised against the Hanafi position that one may give the value (qima) instead of the specific item (shah) in Zakat. The objection claims that allowing value replaces the specific text (nass) with qiyas, thus annulling the text. The Hanafi commentator (likely al-Qamar or al-Sunbuli) responds by clarifying that the change from the text's specified animal to its value is not based on qiyas alone but on the text itself (nass) concerning the guarantee of sustenance for all creatures. The argument involves a deep analysis of the exception ('istithna') in the phrase 'except a case of equality' and the role of 'illah (cause). The commentator shows that the generality of the text was never absolute; it was always limited to situations consistent with the legal standard (al-mi'yar al-shar'i). The cause (ta'lil) only accompanies the text, not overrides it. Further, the passage explains that Zakat is a right of Allah, not the poor, so the lam in 'lil-fuqara' is a lam of consequence ('aqiba), not possession (tamlik). This distinguishes the Hanafi view from the Shafi'i view. The discussion of the hadith of Mu'adh reinforces the principle that Zakat is taken from the rich and given to the poor, but the mechanism of transfer may be through value. The passage is a classic example of Hanafi usul al-fiqh defending the use of qiyas within the bounds of nass.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on shared illah.
النص (al-nass): Explicit textual evidence from Quran or Sunnah; in this passage, the texts about provision and zakat.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): The process of identifying the cause (illah) of a ruling; here the illah for suitability of sheep (being saleable for needs) is used to extend to value.
لام العاقبة (lam al-‘aqiba): The lam of consequence, indicating that the zakat ultimately benefits the poor as a result, though it is initially a right of Allah.
الاستثناء (al-istithna’): Exception; here referring to the exception of 'a case of equality' in the matn, which limits the generality of the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi defense in the passage, why is giving the value (qima) in zakat not an annulment of the text?
A) Because qiyas overrides the text in this particular case.
B) Because the text itself, through its broader indication and exception, permits the substitution.
C) Because the hadith about the sheep is weak and can be ignored.
D) Because the poor prefer value over the sheep.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that giving the value in zakat is permissible is based on the text itself, not on qiyas overriding the text; the text's scope is limited by exceptions and its purpose.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
6 min
The Pillar of Qiyas: Definition and Disputes
Definition and expansion of the pillar of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the rukn of qiyas, explain why the 'illa is a sign not a cause, and distinguish the Iraqi and Samarqandi positions on whether the 'illa is a sign in both the origin and the branch.

Core Idea
The rukn of qiyas is the 'illa, a sign of the ruling; the Iraqi view holds it is a sign only in the branch, not the origin.

This passage defines the essential pillar (rukn) of analogical reasoning (qiyas) in Hanafi usul. It explains that the 'illa (cause) is not a cause in the ontological sense but a sign (علم) placed by the Lawgiver. Inside the Hanafi school, there is a nuanced dispute over whether the 'illa serves as a sign in both the original case (asl) and the new case (far') or only in the new case. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze how analogical reasoning is structured and how textual evidence is prioritized.

Guided Path
1
Define the rukn of qiyas
Identify what the author calls the pillar and why it is called a pillar.
The rukn is 'what is made a sign of the ruling of the text'.
2
Distinguish 'sign' from 'cause'
Explain why the author uses the term 'alam (sign) and not 'cause' - the true effecter is Allah.
The 'illa is an indicator, not a necessitator.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute
Contrast Iraqi and Samarqandi positions on whether the 'illa is a sign in both asl and far' or only in far'.
The Iraqi view is preferred (الظاهر هو الأول).
4
Examine extraction methods
See how the 'illa is either explicit in the text (صيغة) or derived implicitly (بغير صيغة).
Example: inability to deliver from the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam.
5
Formulate the complete definition
Synthesize the four pillars (asl, far', 'illa, hukm) and identify the essential pillar.
The 'illa is the essential pillar; without it, qiyas cannot stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس ۲۸ بيان ركن القياس بيان ركن القياس] وركنه ما جعل علما على حكم النص، وهو المعنى الجامع المسمى علة سمّاه ركنا؛ لأن مدار القياس عليه لا يقوم القياس إلا به، وسماه علماً؛ لأن علل الشرع أمارات ومعرفات للحكم وعلامة عليه، والموجب الحقيقي الله تعالى، وإنما اختلفوا في أن ذلك المعنى هو علم على الحكم في الفرع فقط أم في الأصل أيضًا؟ والظاهر هو الأول على ما ذهب إليه مشايخ العراق؛ لأن النص دليل قطعي، وإضافة الحكم إليه في الأصل أولى من إضافته إلى العلة، وإنما أضيف في الفرع إليها للضرورة حيث لم يوجد فيه النص، وقيل: أضيف حكم الأصل والفرع جميعًا إلى العلة؛ لأنه ما لم يكن لها تأثير في الأصل كيف تؤثر في الفرع. مما اشتمل عليه النص، أي حال كون ذلك العلم ممّا اشتمل عليه النص إمّا صيغةً كاشتمال نص الربا على الكيل والجنس أو بغير صيغة كاشتمال نص النهي عن بيع الآبق على العجز عن التسليم. وجعل الفرع نظيراً له أي للأصل في حكمه بوجوده فيه، أي وجود ذلك المعنى في الفرع ، ويفهم من ههنا أن أركان القياس أربعة الأصل والفرع، والعلة، والحكم، وإن كان أصل الركن هو العلة. مما اشتمل: أي من الأوصاف التي اشتمل إلخ (القمر) نص: أي لفظ مثلاً بمثل. (المحشي) بغير صيغة بأن يكون ذلك المعنى مستنبطا من النص بالالتزام أو بغيره. (القمر) نص النهي إلخ روى الترمذي عن حكيم بن حزام رضي الله عنه قال : نهاني رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن أبيع ما ليس عندي. (القمر) على العجز عن التسليم: فعجز البائع عن التسليم علة للنهي عن بيع الآبق، ولا ذكر لهذا العجز صريحا في نص ذلك النهي إلا أنه مستنبط منه، فإن البيع مذكور فيه ولا بد له من بائع، والعجز صفته، فإذا لم يقدر على التسليم فكيف يتحقق المبادلة. (القمر) وجعل الفرع إلخ قلت احترز به عن المعنى في الدلالة؛ لأن لفظ الفرع يُنبئ عما لا يكون منصوصاً أصلاً، والثابت بمعنى النص في حكم المنصوص. (السنبلي) في حكمه من الحل والحرمة والجواز والفساد (القمر) والعلة أي العلة المشتركة بين الأصل والفرع الموجبة لحكم الأصل. (القمر) والحكم: المراد من الحكم حكم الأصل؛ لأن حكم الفرع ثمرة القياس لتوقفه عليه، ولو كان ركنا من القياس لتوقف على نفسه، وهو باطل (السنبلي) وإن كان أصل الركن إلخ لأن القياس ليس له وجود إلا بالمعنى الذي هو مناط الحكم.(السنبلي) أصل الركن أي الركن الأعظم هو العلة، فإنه ما لم يتحقق العلة لا يتحقق أصل، ولا فرع، ولا حكم (القمر) يدل عليه قول حكيم بن حزام رضي الله عنه: نهاني رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن بيع ما ليس في يدي، رواه الترمذي رقم: ۱۲۳۲، باب ما جاء في كراهية ما ليس عندك.
Chapter of Qiyas. Explanation of the pillar (rukn) of Qiyas. Its pillar is that which is made a sign (علم) of the ruling of the text, and it is the comprehensive meaning called the cause (علة). He called it a pillar because the pivot of qiyas is upon it; qiyas does not stand except by it. And he called it a sign because the causes of the Sharīʿah are indicators and identifiers of the ruling and a mark upon it, while the real necessitator is Allah Almighty. They differed whether that meaning is a sign of the ruling in the branch (فرع) only or also in the origin (أصل)? The apparent is the first, according to the Iraqi mashāyikh, because the text is a definitive proof (دليل قطعي), and attributing the ruling to it in the origin is more appropriate than attributing it to the cause. It is only attributed to the cause in the branch due to necessity, since the text is not found therein. And it was said: The ruling of both origin and branch is attributed to the cause, because if the cause has no effect in the origin, how could it affect the branch? Including what the text contains, i.e., that sign being from what the text contains, either in wording (صيغة) like the text of ribā containing measure (كيل) and genus (جنس), or without wording like the text prohibiting the sale of an absconding slave containing inability to deliver. And making the branch analogous to it (the origin) in its ruling due to the existence of that meaning in the branch. From here it is understood that the pillars of qiyas are four: the origin, the branch, the cause, and the ruling, although the essential pillar is the cause. 'Including what it contains' means from the attributes that it contains... (al-Qamar): 'text' means wording, for example resembling. (al-Muḥashshī): 'without wording' means that the meaning is derived from the text by implication (iltizām) or otherwise. (al-Qamar): The text of the prohibition etc.: al-Tirmidhī narrated from Ḥakīm ibn Ḥizām that he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade me to sell what I do not possess. (al-Qamar): 'Inability to deliver': the seller's inability to deliver is the cause for the prohibition of selling an absconding slave, though there is no explicit mention of this inability in the text of that prohibition; rather it is derived from it, for the sale is mentioned in it and it necessitates a seller, and inability is his attribute; if he cannot deliver, how can exchange be realized? (al-Qamar): 'And making the branch analogous'... I say: he distinguishes by it from the meaning in indication (dalālah), because the term 'branch' indicates what is not textually mentioned at all, and that which is established by the meaning of the text is in the ruling of the textual. (al-Sunbulī): 'In its ruling' from permissibility, prohibition, validity, and invalidity. (al-Qamar): 'The cause' means the common cause between the origin and the branch which necessitates the ruling of the origin. (al-Qamar): 'The ruling': intended from the ruling is the ruling of the origin, because the ruling of the branch is the result of qiyas, due to its dependence on it; if it were a pillar of qiyas, it would depend on itself, which is invalid. (al-Sunbulī): 'And if the essential pillar' etc., because qiyas has no existence except by the meaning which is the basis (manāṭ) of the ruling. (al-Sunbulī): 'The essential pillar' means the greatest pillar is the cause, for if the cause is not realized, neither origin, nor branch, nor ruling is realized. (al-Qamar): This is supported by the saying of Ḥakīm ibn Ḥizām: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade me to sell what is not in my possession, narrated by al-Tirmidhī no. 1232, chapter on the dislike of selling what is not with you.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and analysis of the pillar (rukn) of qiyas (analogical reasoning) from a Hanafi usul work. The author defines the pillar as 'what is made a sign (علم) of the ruling of the text,' which is the comprehensive meaning called the 'illa (cause). It is called a pillar because qiyas pivots on it and cannot exist without it. It is called a sign because the causes of Sharia are indicators (أمارات) and identifiers (معرفات) of the ruling, not the actual necessitators (الموجب الحقيقي هو الله). The author then presents a key dispute: whether the 'illa is a sign of the ruling in the branch (far') only or also in the origin (asl). The preferred view (الظاهر) of the Iraqi mashāyikh is that it is a sign only in the branch, because the origin's ruling is based on a definitive text (نص قطعي), so attributing it directly to the text is more appropriate. The branch's ruling, lacking a direct text, is necessarily attributed to the 'illa. The opposing view (of Samarqandi mashāyikh and most usuliyun) holds that the 'illa is a sign in both, arguing that if it lacks effect in the origin, it cannot affect the branch. The passage then explains that the 'illa may be explicitly found in the text (e.g., measurement and genus in ribā) or implicitly derived (e.g., inability to deliver from the prohibition of selling an absconding slave). The hadith of Ḥakīm ibn Ḥizām is cited as evidence for the latter: the Prophet forbade selling what one does not possess, and inability to deliver is the underlying cause. The passage concludes by stating that the pillars of qiyas are four (asl, far', 'illa, hukm), but the essential pillar is the 'illa.

Key Terms
ركن القياس (rukn al-qiyās): The essential component of qiyas without which qiyas cannot exist; identified as the comprehensive meaning (المعنى الجامع) called the 'illa (cause).
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause that is a sign (علم) of the Sharia ruling; it is the basis upon which analogical extension is made.
علم (ʿalam): A sign or indicator; here it refers to the 'illa as a marker of the ruling, not a cause in the ontological sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Iraqi Hanafi mashāyikh, why is the 'illa considered a sign (علم) of the ruling only in the branch (far') and not in the origin (asl)?
A) Because the origin's ruling is based on a definitive text, so attributing it to the text is stronger.
B) Because the origin lacks a cause altogether.
C) Because the 'illa is not derived from the origin.
D) Because the branch is more important than the origin.

Key Takeaway
The rukn of qiyas is the 'illa, a sign of the ruling, and the preferred Hanafi view is that it functions as a sign only in the branch due to the primacy of the definitive text in the origin.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Pillars of Qiyas and the Role of the Cause
Defines the four pillars of qiyas with focus on the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four pillars of qiyas and explain the Hanafi position on attributing the ruling to the text or the cause.

Core Idea
The cause is the greatest pillar of qiyas; without it, analogy has no foundation.

In this passage from Noor al-Anwar, the author explains the essential components of qiyas (analogical reasoning). While qiyas has four pillars—the original case, the new case, the common cause, and the ruling—the most fundamental is the cause. Understanding how Hanafis attribute rulings to either the text or the cause is crucial for mastering usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four pillars
From the text, list the four pillars of qiyas: the original (asl), the branch (far'), the cause (illah), and the ruling (hukm). Note that the passage says the original pillar is the cause.
The pillars are not all equal; the cause is primary.
2
Understand attribution in original vs. branch
The Hanafi scholars of Iraq hold that in the original case, the ruling is attributed to the text because the text is a decisive proof. In the branch, because no text exists, the ruling is attributed to the cause due to necessity.
This is a key point of ikhtilaf with other schools.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rationale
If the cause had no effect in the original, it could not affect the branch. But the Hanafis maintain that the text remains the ultimate source; attributing to the cause in the branch is for methodological clarity.
Some Hanafis also attribute both to the cause, but the preferred view is the one above.
4
Examine examples of cause derivation
The cause may be explicit in wording (e.g., riba: measurement and type) or implicit, derived by implication (e.g., sale of an absconding slave: inability to deliver). The hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam is cited to illustrate an implicit cause.
The hadith prohibits selling what one does not possess; the cause is inability to deliver.
5
Evaluate the dispute
Some scholars attribute the ruling solely to the cause, arguing that if the cause had no effect in the original, it cannot affect the branch. The Hanafi response is that the original's ruling is based on the text, and the cause is used only for the branch due to the absence of text.
The practical difference (thamarah) affects how qiyas is applied when both text and cause are present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس
