Chapter of Qiyas
45
Exposition of the invalidity of the description which...
With less than a verse; because it is not called Qur'an in custom even if it is called so in language. Due to the presence of recitation. And arguing without evidence is conjuncted to the previous, i.e., like uniformity in invalidity is arguing without evidence.
For the purpose of negation, by saying: this ruling is not established because there is no evidence for it. If he claims it is not established in the mind of the arguer, then there is no doubt about its permissibility; because his not finding evidence necessitates his not finding the ruling in his knowledge. And if he claims it is not established in reality due to not finding evidence for it, then they differed on it: It is said it is permissible based on His statement: {Say: I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden...} (al-An‘ām 6:145) For He taught His Prophet arguing by 'I do not find' as evidence for its non-prohibition. And it is said it is permissible in shar‘ī matters but not in rational matters; because the claimant of negation and affirmation in rational matters claims the reality of existence and non-existence, so he must provide evidence, and lack of evidence is insufficient, contrary to shar‘ī matters; for they are not like that. And according to the majority (jumhūr), it is not a proof at all, neither in negation nor in affirmation.
Language: i.e., by the Qur'an because recitation is present in it as well. (al-Muḥashshī on the previous i.e., his statement: ta'līl by negation (al-Qamar) By saying: i.e., the mujtahid after complete research and investigation when he does not find evidence for this ruling etc. (al-Qamar) And if he claims it is not etc. i.e., he says or believes it is not from Allah. (al-Qamar) It is said: the one who says so is some of the Shāfi‘īs, among them al-Qāḍī al-Bayḍāwī, it is said. (al-Qamar) Forbidden: i.e., food forbidden to a person who eats it except if it is carrion or spilled blood... (al-An‘ām 6:145) the verse. (al-Qamar) For He taught His Prophet etc. And we say: arguing without evidence from the Lawgiver is valid; because His knowledge encompasses the evidences, and He is the Legislator of rulings and the Placer of evidences, so His testimony on the lack of evidence requiring prohibition is decisive proof of the lack of evidence, for the Lawgiver is neither forgetful nor incapable, unlike humans, for forgetfulness and incapacity exist in them. Thus said the author in his commentary. (al-Qamar) On its non-prohibition i.e., the prohibition of food other than the exceptions. (al-Qamar)
It is incumbent on them, not in rational matters: i.e., the claimant of negation must produce evidence in rational matters, but not in shar‘ī matters. (al-Qamar) They are not like that i.e., shar‘ī matters are not like rational matters; their basis is transmission. (al-Qamar) And according to the majority (jumhūr) i.e., from our scholars and the Shāfi‘īs, it is not a proof at all. For not finding evidence does not necessitate the non-existence of evidence in reality nor the non-existence of the indicated (madlūl) in it. So if the mujtahid after thorough research does not find evidence for a ruling, he says: there is no ruling from the Lawgiver regarding it, neither negation nor affirmation; he does not say that the negation of this ruling is from the Lawgiver, because there is no evidence for it. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Qiyas (analogy) addresses two types of flawed arguments in legal reasoning: (1) using a description that is evidently corrupt as a basis for analogy, and (2) arguing from the mere absence of evidence (iḥtijāj bi-lā dalīl). The author first critiques a Shāfi‘ī analogy regarding the validity of a slave's writing contract (kitāba) with wine. The Shāfi‘īs argued that immediate writing is invalid by analogy with writing for wine, claiming both writings do not prevent the master from expiating a vow (takfīr). The Hanafīs respond that the invalidity of the wine contract is due to wine not being valuable property, not because it does not prevent expiation. According to the Hanafī school, writing (whether immediate or deferred) does not prevent expiation at all before any payment. Thus, the Shāfi‘īs must prove that deferred writing prevents expiation to draw a valid analogy—something they cannot do. This shows that using a description that is clearly not the true cause (illah) makes the analogy invalid. The second topic concerns arguing without evidence, i.e., claiming a ruling is unestablished because no evidence is found. The text presents three views: some Shāfi‘īs (e.g., al-Qāḍī al-Bayḍāwī) allow it, citing the Prophet's use of 'I do not find' in the Qur'an (6:145) to argue against prohibition. Others restrict it to shar‘ī matters (where reliance on transmitted evidence is key), not rational matters. The majority (including both Hanafīs and Shāfi‘īs) hold it is not a proof at all—absence of evidence does not prove absence of ruling; the mujtahid can only say he has found no ruling, not that the ruling is negated by the Lawgiver. Throughout, the commentary clarifies that the Hanafī position on the kitāba issue is that both immediate and deferred writing do not prevent expiation (a ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah as per al-Durr al-Mukhtār). The discussion on arguing without evidence touches on key usul categories: the validity of negative claims (al-‘adam al-aṣlī) and the burden of proof.

Key Terms
احتجاج بلا دليل (iḥtijāj bi-lā dalīl): Arguing that a ruling is unestablished simply because no evidence is found for it; this is debated in usul.
عدم وجدان الدليل (‘adam wijdān al-dalīl): The act of not finding evidence after thorough search. This is not equivalent to the non-existence of evidence in reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the jumhūr (majority of Hanafīs and Shāfi‘īs), if a mujtahid does not find evidence for a ruling, he should:
A) Conclude that the ruling is negated by the Lawgiver.
B) Say 'I have not found evidence for this ruling' without claiming its non-existence.
C) Apply iḥtijāj bi-lā dalīl and declare the ruling non-existent.
D) Conclude that the ruling is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The majority of Hanafīs and Shāfi‘īs reject iḥtijāj bi-lā dalīl as a proof; a mujtahid can only report his own lack of finding, not the absence of a ruling in reality.
